  • Also: US–China AI talks that fell through in May are back on for September (awkward, given the escalating distillation feud) · a judge approved Anthropic's record $1.5B settlement over using authors' books to train its AI (not everyone considers it a win) · analysts say Nvidia is quietly buying fiber-optic cable across the US, with capacity to theoretically carry several times the world's internet traffic · more than 50% of all web traffic is now nonhuman
💡 Big-picture link

This is the hardware answer to Jul 17's price war. When model prices collapse (Kimi K3), you defend margin by driving inference costs down — that's Frozen, Broadcom and Samsung. But it's simultaneously a frontal threat to Nvidia's general-purpose GPU dominance, and it must be read next to TSMC raising prices 10% the same day: margin is migrating from the model layer down to silicon. And China's export-control discussion reveals, within three days, that Jul 18's "public good" was a strategy, not a conviction.

K-Shape
(7/21–22) "The market only benefits the top 1%" — two in five Americans say so, and the top 1% do own half the market
Brew Markets (Harris/Guardian · Fed) · Morning Brew ▾
The K shape isn't just for the economy any more. A Harris poll run by The Guardian found two out of five Americans believe the stock market only benefits the wealthiest 1%. ⚠️ And the data says they may have a point — per the Federal Reserve, the top 1% of households by income own 50% of the stock market. Worse: one third of respondents said they'd see better financial results from gambling than from the stock market.
  • Miami is now more expensive than New York (for the first time ever, on 2024 government data): consumer prices are +36% for Miamians since 2019, more than any US metro except Tampa. Home prices +79% since the pandemic, property taxes +62%, an average restaurant check of $94 vs $79 in New York. ⚠️ Yet Miami workers earn $1,000 less per year than the median American — the exact opposite of what transplants came for. Meanwhile billionaires keep relocating (Ken Griffin's $107M estate this year; Zuckerberg, Brin and Page)
  • IPO returns point the direction: biotech and pharma companies that listed this year are up 55% on average — against 4.4% for all 2026 IPOs and a 6% average loss for the year's ten biggest, including AI chipmakers and SpaceX. Veradermics (hair loss) is +500%; Hemab +100%. Looser regulation, research breakthroughs and cash-flush Big Pharma buying are behind it
  • The $100B weight-loss war: Novo Nordisk sued Eli Lilly over its GLP-1 ads, saying Lilly compares its highest approved doses against lower doses of Ozempic and Wegovy while omitting newer versions — calling the ads "maliciously and deceptively false." Lilly stands by them: "Rather than compete on the merits of its products, Novo is asking a court to stop Lilly from communicating the results of that trial." Lilly +2.58%, Novo −0.51% (YTD +9.35% vs −3.61%). The next front is pills, where Novo leads (oral Wegovy 16.6% weight loss over 64 weeks vs Lilly's Foundayo 12.4% over 72). The market is seen at $100B by 2030
  • Also: Apple is partnering with Klarna to let you lease iPhones (Apple Upgrade, US rollout Jul 28) — buy-now-pay-later comes to the iPhone · ESPN cut on-air names (Ryan Clark, Cam Newton) in the NFL Network merger, while Disney cut hundreds more at Pixar and National Geographic
💡 Big-picture link

Jul 16's "66% can't afford groceries" has evolved into "the market itself isn't ours." That a third would rather gamble shares a root with Jul 16's Kalshi insider trading and clinical-trial betting — prediction markets filling the space vacated by trust in institutional finance. And biotech IPOs +55% versus −6% for the biggest AI listings is the first hard reading on Authers' question: where does the bubble roll next?

July 21, 2026
Tue — momentum's biggest reverse since the pandemic, a new UK PM, 50% tariffs on Canada, gasoline back above $4
Jul 21
Momentum
(7/21) "Big Mo takes a beating" — momentum's biggest reverse since the pandemic, with Asian retail at the epicenter
Bloomberg (John Authers) ▾
Authers identified what this week's SOX bear market actually is — not a semiconductor story but the collapse of the momentum factor. The US indexes still sit near record highs, but the plates just beneath the surface moved dramatically. Every big US chip name is down 20%+ this month — often called a "bear market," which is absurd when they're still +60% over 12 months, but a dramatic reverse all the same.
  • It was easy to see coming: in his July 1 "Hindsight Capital" column, Authers flagged semis' extreme outperformance of software as unsustainable. "The reversal started just as the month turned — and it was good to get it over with swiftly." Nothing inherently unhealthy about it
  • ⚠️ The issue is the momentum factor: "buy the winners, sell/short the laggards" is the backbone of many strategies, but you must endure occasional drastic reverses. Informed Momentum (Prentice): "take the steps up, be ready to take the elevator down." The rally began in late March on Gulf-ceasefire hopes → by June stocks paused while momentum blazed ahead = the hint something unsustainable was afoot
  • The real epicenter is Asian retail: surprisingly, China saw just as big a momentum surge as the US and took an even nastier July reverse — Big Mo's biggest setback since the pandemic. The cause is retail investors. Leveraged single-stock ETFs on big chipmakers are especially alarming — short-term speculation aimed at retail (hedge funds already have other tools). Retail presence amplifies volume, momentum and volatility → which is why Asia reversed harder (SocGen's Lapthorne)
  • Europe is the shelter: Lapthorne — "Europe looks almost sleepy… momentum strategies have been far more resilient there. If you're looking for shelter from the collapse in retail-driven momentum, Europe looks like one of the safer harbors."
  • ⚠️ Trouble for quants: under Trump 2.0, momentum has underperformed a strategy of buying the most-shorted and shorting the least-shorted. The big chips are the least-shorted US stocks, so it follows — but trend-follower losses + short-seller reverses = bad times for quant funds. Coming right after a Russell rebalance, it's unclear whether this is a new trend or a "natural correction to let off steam"
💡 Big-picture link

The "why" of Jul 17's SOX bear market is completed here. This report already wrote that day that it was "most crowded trade + collapsing pricing power + leverage = forced liquidation" — and Authers reveals the identity of that leverage: Asian retail's single-stock ETFs. Korea's 300,000 liquidated accounts weren't a coincidence; Asia was structurally bound to be the epicenter. And May 1's "the danger is when everyone holds the same position" is now academically confirmed as a collapse of the momentum factor. What to watch: whether the reverse gathers its own speed and inflicts serious losses on quants and retail.

UK Politics
(7/21) The UK's new PM Andy Burnham — the seventh in a decade, as gilt yields spike above 5% in "warning"
Bloomberg (John Authers) · Morning Brew ▾
Andy Burnham entered 10 Downing Street as the UK's seventh PM in a decade. Authers' read: the best communicator in the top job since Blair (Boris aside), who must now end a damaging streak of short premierships. The bond market greeted him with a +8.1bp move in the 10-year gilt, back above 5% — higher than it reached under Truss, and the highest since 2008.
  • The Truss lesson: felled seven weeks in by a gilt revolt against unfunded tax cuts, a discipline that now binds. Gilts once yielded the same as bunds; the gap is now ~2 percentage points — a problem for any PM who wants to spend
  • John Healey named chancellor in a total blindside (Miliband and Mahmood were the favorites; Polymarket missed it): no relation to 1970s Labour chancellor Denis Healey. A former Treasury minister under Brown, most recently the defense secretary whose resignation damaged Starmer — presumably assured he could hike the defense budget before taking the job
  • Authers' conclusion: like Blair winning years of leeway by making the BOE independent in his first week (1997), this is a surgical strike to win investors' trust. "Healey doesn't want to be Denis; Burnham is doing his utmost not to be Liz Truss."
  • Burnham promised "a new political and economic model" and re-nationalizing water and energy (May inflation 2.8%). Stronger defense + keeping bond markets onside = less money for other popular projects
💡 Big-picture link

Another axis of the week — fiscal discipline. While US inflation is re-igniting on oil and tariffs, in Britain 5% gilts are a leash on the new PM from day one. It's the same picture as Warsh's Fed (Jul 14's "not mission accomplished"): a regime in which central banks and bond markets constrain politics' fiscal expansion at the source.

Trade / War
(7/21) 50% tariffs on Canada, day ten of the Iran war · the Houthis open a Red Sea front, gasoline back to $4
Morning Brew · Bloomberg (John Authers) ▾
Trump signed an additional 50% tariff on many Canadian goods (wine, hockey sticks, plywood, furniture) — a major escalation against the #2 trading partner (effective in 30 days, no USMCA exemption). Meanwhile day ten of the Iran war, and the Houthis threatened to blockade Saudi ships in the Red Sea, opening a new front. US gasoline is back to $4 a gallon (vs $3.14 a year ago).
  • The Iran war: ten straight days, with ~100 US service members injured in two weeks. After three US soldiers were killed over the weekend, Trump vowed Iran "will pay many times over." The Houthi Red Sea threat is a third chokepoint after Jul 16's Bab el-Mandeb. But Iran said mediators floated a new ceasefire proposal — the negotiation channel is alive
  • Gasoline $4: the consumer face of Jul 18's $90 Brent. The Jul 16 "66% can't afford groceries" vs Jul 18 five-month-high sentiment contradiction is being re-tightened by oil — the Michigan survey's "oil could make it short-lived" caveat is now real
  • Paramount–WBD $110B merger paused: a California judge granted a 14-day restraining order (a 12-state antitrust challenge) — a signal of regulatory risk amid AI/media consolidation
  • Google rose +1.50% on reports it's developing a new AI chip, ahead of Wednesday's Alphabet earnings. The Nasdaq was flat (−0.05%), pinned by oil volatility
💡 Big-picture link

Three chokepoints (Hormuz, Bab el-Mandeb, now the Red Sea) are under simultaneous pressure — Papic's "oil is an independent variable; the war can't settle itself" spreading geographically. And hitting Canada with 50% three days before the July 24 China-tariff deadline signals tariffs re-broadening on all fronts. Gasoline at $4 is where all of it lands — in the wallet at the bottom of the K.

July 17–20, 2026
Fri–Mon — the SOX enters a bear market, Apple takes the crown, and SpaceX loses $1T in a month
Jul 17–20
Semis Bear Market
(7/17) The day the forecast came true — Kimi K3 drops, the SOX enters a bear market, and 300,000 Korean accounts are liquidated
Brew Markets · Finimize · Morning Brew ▾
Exactly what yesterday warned about happened. Moonshot's Kimi K3 dropped claiming to outperform GPT-5.5 and Claude Opus 4.8 on several coding and agent benchmarks, and AI stocks slid across the board. The PHLX Semiconductor Index (SOX) entered a bear market, −20% from its June peak (before bouncing) — Nasdaq −1.40%, S&P −1.01%. It was the worst week for US chip stocks in over a year (SOX −8.5% on the week).
  • The hardest hit were the year's biggest winners: Marvell and Sandisk are down 30%+ this month. A long-standing investor worry — that the AI-spending boom can't last forever — collided with price weakness and oversupply fears as chipmakers add capacity
  • ⚠️ Momentum betrayed — Korea was the epicenter: when everyone holds the same position and rushes for the exit at once, selling gets brutal — especially with leverage. Weeks of Kospi declines pushed 1.2M+ retail accounts toward margin calls, and at least 300,000 were fully liquidated — that forced selling drove Korean tech lower and spread into a global-tech (US included) rush for the exits (a feedback loop)
  • Authers' frame (A Market Definition of Breadth): this fits the "rolling bubble" thesis — AI isn't one bubble but froth moving from sector to sector. The torch passed from hyperscalers to semis, and the next question is whether fund managers managed their semi exposure as well as their Mag 7 exposure. Trivariate: Apple is the year's best Magnificent yet only 134th in the S&P — bad news for long-only managers who expected to outperform by underweighting it
  • The other side, same day: gold rebounded ($4,014, +0.55%) as traders decided it "looks cheap now." Oil rose +4.15% to $81.53 (US–Iran fighting; Kuwait targeted by an Iranian strike)
💡 Big-picture link

The forecast came true within 48 hours. Two days ago (Jul 15) the BofA survey said "semis = most crowded ever"; yesterday (Jul 17) Tech Brew flagged the "open-weight price war"; today the SOX enters a bear market. Three items chained into cause and effect in three days — most crowded trade + collapsing pricing power + leverage = forced liquidation. And May 1's note — "the danger is when everyone holds the same position" — took concrete form in 300,000 Korean accounts. The rolling bubble has started to pop in the semiconductor square.

Crown Change
(7/17–18) Apple takes the crown — passing Nvidia as the world's most valuable company for the first time in over a year ($4.88T)
Brew Markets · Finimize · Morning Brew ▾
No dynasty lasts forever. Apple passed Nvidia intraday to become the world's largest company — the first time since last April. At the close, Apple $4.88T vs Nvidia $4.86T. Apple is the best Mag 7 performer this year at +22.76%; Nvidia has managed just +8.75%.
  • HSBC upgraded Apple to Buy from Hold, and the basis is the money it isn't spending. Apple's 2026 AI investment is just 2.5% of revenue — versus a hyperscaler average of 39%. Instead it's aiming for a big upgrade cycle with a smarter Siri and a September foldable iPhone. With the Jul 16 China-approval news on top, it's being repriced as a company that profits from AI rather than spends on it
  • Nvidia's slide: IBM blamed weak results on customers redirecting budgets to memory (Micron +197%, Sandisk +471% YTD); now Nvidia is caught in the same rotation. It fell 2.21% on the Kimi K3 news
  • ⚠️ There are skeptics: only 60% of analysts rate Apple a Buy — versus ~90% for Microsoft, Amazon, Meta and Nvidia. Authers (citing Trivariate): Apple is the best Magnificent yet only 134th in the S&P, prompting the question "why doesn't this feel better?" — managers who underweighted hyperscalers feel they should have won by more
  • Finimize: the race is a razor-thin $4.90T contest and a sign attention is shifting from AI-chip leadership to Apple's services, Siri and September CEO succession
💡 Big-picture link

Apple's story is now a five-peat and a peak — Jul 13 the money it didn't spend → Jul 15 Qwen and a record → Jul 16 Siri "Signal" + China approval → Jul 17 the crown reclaimed. And it's the final proof that the definition of the AI trade has changed: the crown passed from "the company that pours money into AI" to "the company that profits from it." Nvidia losing the crown is itself the proof that the SOX bear market, Kimi K3 and IBM −25% were one and the same.

SpaceX
(7/18) SpaceX loses $1T in a month — a scrubbed launch plus a broken IPO price, and short sellers make $4B
Morning Brew ▾
It dreams of Mars but looks very earthbound to investors right now. The stock tumbled after Thursday's Starship launch was scrubbed. Its market cap fell from $2.64T on June 16 (its third-day close) to $1.63T yesterday — roughly $1 trillion gone in a month. Short sellers made about $4 billion on the bet.
  • The stock that popped on June's record IPO recently slipped below its issue price. Its first launch since going public was a key test for the newly public company — and the scrub postponed the test
  • Why it's symbolic: the core of June's IPO story was "SpaceX is an AI company" (the Colossus compute deal). That story hit its trial in the same week as the SOX bear market and Kimi K3 — a live experiment in what happens when "a bet on a loss-making future" meets a physical failure (a scrubbed launch)
💡 Big-picture link

June's conclusion — "a market experiment in betting on a loss-making future, with the answer coming back on July 15 as a broken IPO price" — is now complete, at $1T erased. It's the largest instance of the same story as Jul 16's "two-thirds of energy IPOs below issue price": the phase where anything touching AI could list has fully given way to the phase where it must prove itself in the physical world.

China AI
(7/18) The openness reversal — Xi calls AI "a global public good," and Chinese models are 60%+ of OpenRouter traffic
Finimize · Morning Brew ▾
Xi Jinping appeared in person for the first time at Shanghai's AI conference to cast AI as "a global public good the whole world shares," calling for "a symphony of global cooperation" and criticizing US restrictions on AI tech to China. He pledged 5,000 AI courses to developing nations over five years, and a new World AI Cooperation Organization has 29 signatories. ⚠️ But with no mention of fresh government investment, Chinese AI and chip stocks fell.
  • A dramatic role reversal: for decades the US was "open" (the open internet, open source, global platforms) while China sat behind the Great Firewall. Now China releases AI as a downloadable digital public good, while the US guards frontier models with export controls, security rules and closed platforms. It's strategic — make models easy to download and dirt cheap to win over developers worldwide and erode US AI ambitions
  • It's already working: Chinese models are 60%+ of global traffic on the AI marketplace OpenRouter, ahead of the US. Even US firms — Airbnb, Pinterest and DoorDash are evaluating Chinese models, and even Microsoft is considering DeepSeek for Copilot Cowork. The reason is simple: Anthropic's Claude Fable 5 costs over 100x more per standard task than DeepSeek V4 Flash
  • Kimi K3 specs (Moonshot's benchmarks): it outperforms every available model except two — Claude Fable 5 and GPT-5.6 Sol. Parameters: 2.8T (about double Opus 4.8's estimated 1.5T). Price: $15 per 1M output tokens vs $30 for GPT-5.6 Sol and $50 for Claude Fable 5. Open-sourcing planned for later this month
  • The fairness fight: Anthropic accuses Alibaba (a Moonshot investor) of trying to extract Claude's capabilities to train its own models; OpenAI makes a similar claim about DeepSeek. Déjà vu of last year's DeepSeek shock
💡 Big-picture link

This is the geopolitical version of Jul 17's price war. It isn't mere competition — it's a reversal of national open/closed strategy: on Jun 15 the US blocked non-US access to Anthropic's models (borders for AI), and China does the opposite by erasing borders. And the single line that Fable 5 costs 100x more than DeepSeek is a direct threat to the trillion-dollar valuations on the OpenAI and Anthropic IPOs. May 1's forecast → Jul 17's price war → Jul 18, that war became a US–China contest of systems.

Iran War
(7/18–20) Day seven of fighting — oil tops $90 for the first time in six weeks, Iraq and Syria agree a Hormuz-bypass pipeline
Morning Brew · Bloomberg (John Authers) ▾
Trump's "the truce is over" wasn't idle — the US and Iran have fought for seven straight days. The US struck bridges and energy facilities; Iran hit US regional allies (Bahrain, Kuwait). Brent topped $90 for the first time in six weeks (early in the session), and Hormuz throughput hit a three-week low. The war has now cost a 17th US service member since it began on Feb 28.
  • Workarounds are appearing: Iraq and Syria agreed to rebuild a pipeline — an alternative for moving crude that bypasses Hormuz. Experts caution Iran remains a regional threat. After the Jul 16 Bab el-Mandeb threat, this is the first attempt to physically route logistics around the chokepoint
  • Michigan consumer sentiment rose to a five-month high (54.4), but ⚠️ with a caveat that Iran-driven oil gains could make the improvement short-lived. It collides head-on with Jul 16's "66% can't afford groceries" — the gap between sentiment and the physical economy
  • Authers' observation (WFH and offices): New York commuting is still a third below pre-pandemic — a seemingly permanent behavioral shift. Office REITs have fallen further since ChatGPT launched (remote work entrenched). Authers' question: just as there was a backlash to social media and AI, will there be one to WFH — fast enough to rescue office real estate?
💡 Big-picture link

Jul 13 reclosure → Jul 15 strikes → Jul 16 Bab el-Mandeb → Jul 18 day seven, $90, a bypass pipeline. The war is hardening from a shock into a structure. And the week's completed irony: Michigan sentiment is at a five-month high while 66% can't afford groceries; oil is at $90 while gold and stocks wobble. Papic is reconfirmed — oil is an independent variable, and this war does not settle itself.

Earnings Breadth
(7/20) "Feel the breadth, not the beat" — the real question for Q2 is whether it extends beyond AI
Bloomberg (John Authers) ▾
Authers: Q2 beating estimates is a foregone conclusion and therefore doesn't matter (earnings management makes it so). The real question is whether corporate momentum is as strong as it looks, and whether it extends beyond the AI winners. This week, Alphabet and Tesla open the Mag 7 season on Wednesday.
  • The evidence for breadth: Deutsche Bank (Binky Chadha) sees the "rest of the S&P 500" — ex-tech and AI — growing earnings +14.3% (contributing 9.1pp of total growth). ISM manufacturing is back in expansion. BofA (Subramanian): ISM alone implies 10% Q2 growth and services PMI implies 20% — both short of the 23.4% consensus, and that gap is precisely tech's excess contribution
  • The consumer holds up: ClearBridge (Jamner) notes consumer-staples and retail expectations have reversed from early-year pessimism — with the caveat, "if the labor market cools markedly, that's when we change our view." Valuation: since ChatGPT (2022) the forward P/E has held around 20x — earnings have done the heavy lifting (NTM expectations +155%)
  • The rolling bubble's next question: in Authers' second piece — the Mag 7 hyperscalers have lagged the average stock this year and the torch has passed to semis. ⚠️ The paradox: if hyperscalers now prove their capex is bearing fruit, that's bad news for underweight long-only managers. The next question is where the bubble rolls, and whether managers handled their semi exposure as well as their Mag 7 exposure
  • This week's calendar: Wed Alphabet and Tesla (Alphabet is spending up to $190B on data centers this year = 6x 2022, with FCF −70%), Thu Intel and SAP, Fri the July 24 tariff deadline (the 150-day Section 122 expiry). And on Monday, Andy Burnham became the UK's new PM (the seventh in a decade)
💡 Big-picture link

The week's two narratives collide here. The bull case (Authers on breadth): if earnings extend beyond AI, the rally shifts from "exceptionalism" to "a healthy base." The bear case (this week's physical events): the SOX bear market, Kimi K3 and Korea's forced liquidation show the most crowded trade is unwinding. Wednesday's Alphabet capex ($190B) and Tesla margins are the first verdict — if the capex bears fruit the bulls win, but paradoxically the semi-underweight managers get hurt. The July 24 tariff deadline sits on top of it all.

July 16–17, 2026
Thu–Fri — the crowded trade sends its bill within 48 hours, Iran's second chokepoint, Americans stop buying groceries, and an AI price war
Jul 16
Rotation
(7/16) Nasdaq −1.47% — "one of those days when they remember they're kinda scared of the AI boom." And gold broke $4,000
Brew Markets · Morning Brew · Finimize ▾
Yesterday's "out of chips, into Big Tech" became, in a single session, "out of chips, full stop." The tech sector pulled the entire index down with it — Nasdaq 25,881.95 (−1.47%), S&P 7,533.77 (−0.51%), Dow 52,552.97 (−0.20%). Morning Brew's framing: stocks tumbled as investors decided it was one of those days when they remember they're kinda scared of the AI boom. The 10-year backed up to 4.569% (+2.0bps).
  • Chips took it: Intel −5.84% ($96.98), continuing a month-long slide. TSMC −2.32% — despite a 77% jump in quarterly profit. TSMC said advanced packaging is still tight and it will lift capex, but hedged that the Arizona buildout depends on market demand. Meanwhile The Information reports ASML wants to raise equipment prices despite pushback from its biggest customer, TSMC
  • Alphabet −4.43% — for delaying the release of its new AI model. The price tag for being one beat late in the model race is now public
  • SpaceX −3.08% — ahead of tonight's rocket launch, its first since the IPO and a key test for the newly public company. A second straight down day after breaking its issue price
  • Gold lost $4,000 — spot gold fell below $4,000/oz for the first time since last November (−1.77% to $3,980). The mystery of the day: the war is escalating and the hedge got sold
  • Visa voted for crypto: a new platform letting institutions "access, store, and redeem stablecoins" — a vote of confidence from a major financial institution and a direct threat to Circle, which slid 7.66%
  • Earnings split: UnitedHealth +1.2% on a blowout Q2 and raised guidance, helped by easing medical costs and AI-driven efficiency gains · Abbott +10.69% · ManpowerGroup +32.37% · J.B. Hunt +8.01% · Uber +1.89% on a $14.8B deal for Delivery Hero. Against them: GE Aerospace −3.47% (a strong report that didn't clear high expectations) · United Airlines −1.79% despite a beat, on warning of $6B in extra jet fuel costs this year · AST SpaceMobile −17.04% ($1B convertible) · Netflix −9% after hours — earnings matched, but it narrowed its 2026 revenue forecast
💡 Big-picture link

BofA's survey sent its bill within 48 hours. The day after a poll showing 80%+ calling semis the most crowded trade ever recorded, TSMC fell on a 77% profit jump. Good results that can't lift a stock mean everyone already owns it. The awareness Authers called "good news" may now be turning into actual selling.

Bab el-Mandeb
(7/16) Iran's second chokepoint — "if the US hits our power infrastructure, the Houthis shut Bab el-Mandeb"
Finimize · Brew Markets ▾
Yesterday's threat about "other export routes" got coordinates within 24 hours. Tehran told the Houthis to prepare to shut the Bab el-Mandeb strait — conditional on the US striking Iranian power infrastructure. Brent pushed above $85 in the morning before giving up the gains to close at $84.78 (−0.20%); WTI settled at $78.43 (−0.87%).
  • Why this is a new phase: Hormuz is a door Iran closes itself. Bab el-Mandeb is a door a proxy closes — meaning Iran has acquired a second valve while diffusing the responsibility for escalation. If the Red Sea–Suez route goes too, everything bound for Europe reroutes around the Cape
  • The conditionality is the point: this isn't a closure, it's a deterrent — a price tag attached in advance to a US strike on the grid, engineered as the counter to Jul 15's stated US objective of degrading Iran's ability to attack shipping
  • The market reaction is strange: oil rose in the morning and gave it back by the afternoon, and gold broke below $4,000 the same day. The war escalates and the war hedges get sold — the market is still betting this escalation is short-lived, exactly the premise embedded in BofA's "dollar is overvalued, little upside left" (Jul 15)
  • Finimize Pro cut its Hormuz Recovery Basket — opened May 26 with eight names, now seven. It had added to the position in mid-June as tanker traffic resumed and the ceasefire looked steadier; last week's resumption of strikes broke the premise. The whole thesis — durable ceasefire → lower oil → easier inflation → better supply chains — is being rewritten
💡 Big-picture link

Jul 13 reclosure → Jul 15 strikes and "other routes" → Jul 16 Bab el-Mandeb named. The escalation ladder just grew a rung. Yet the same day oil fell and gold lost $4,000 — the week's central dissonance. Physical risk is rising while asset prices keep assuming June's peace. Papic's warning reasserts itself here: when oil falls, both sides get bolder.

Consumer
(7/16–17) Americans' money-saving hack: not buying groceries — 66% say they're unaffordable, up from 45% in February
Morning Brew · Brew Markets ▾
Under mild economic duress, consumers cut discretionary spending. When they stop buying groceries, it's a different story. Per a new Bain & Company analysis of NielsenIQ data, June grocery unit sales — individual items sold — fell 1.8% year-on-year. In June 2025 they rose 0.1%.
  • Washington Post–Ipsos: 66% of US shoppers say groceries are unaffordable — a steep climb from 45% in February. That's 21 points in five months
  • USAA survey: 35% of Americans are using credit card reward points to pay for everyday items like food and gas. More are redeeming points for cash than usual; fewer are spending them on travel or big-ticket treats — rewards have been repurposed from funding luxuries to covering necessities
  • Why now: grocery prices are about 33% higher than seven years ago. Inflation running 2–4% for years compounded, and SNAP cuts hit lower-income Americans directly. Shoppers are trying couponing, deal-hunting and swapping name brands for private label
  • Who wins and loses: consumer staples that normally ride out downturns — PepsiCo among them — may find themselves in trouble, because the consumer abandons the brand. The flip side favours Walmart and anyone promising household essentials at a discount exactly when shoppers want a deal
  • ⚠️ And even though June's inflation data was softer than economists expected, energy prices — which got relief last month — are expected to keep rising amid renewed tensions with Iran. In other words, these surveys don't yet reflect the worst of it
💡 Big-picture link

Two of this week's data points describe the same person — Jul 15 Schroders: 69% say their generation can't retire; Jul 16 WaPo: 66% can't afford groceries. And they landed in the very week CPI's first fall since 2020 set off the celebrations. On the bottom leg of the K, the gap between the price index and lived experience has hit a threshold — and that's the fuel behind Dimon's "anti-rich" resentment. No landing is a story about the top leg.

Pharma
(7/16) The patent cliff is coming — Merck gets an oral cholesterol pill, Lilly pays $2.8B for psychedelics
Brew Markets · Morning Brew ▾
A cholesterol pill and magic mushrooms don't seem to have much in common, but both could be Big Pharma's next cash cow — and both were pushed on the same day for one reason: the patent cliff.
  • Merck wins FDA approval (+3.29%): Lipfendra — a groundbreaking oral drug that treats high cholesterol even better than statins, the current gold standard. It targets and removes PCSK9, the protein that stops the body clearing LDL. The mechanism already exists as an injection (Amgen's Repatha, $3B a year), where cost has been the barrier to wider adoption. Merck's proposed price is far lower, and a pill is more palatable — the WSJ sees $5B a year
  • Eli Lilly buys AtaiBeckley for $2.8B (+1.19%), with another $1B on milestones, gaining mental-health therapies built on DMT and MDMA. The lead asset is BPL-003, a DMT-based nasal spray for treatment-resistant depression with promising mid-stage results and an FDA Breakthrough Therapy designation last fall; also an oral drug for social anxiety built on a molecular structure found in MDMA
  • Why psychedelics, why now — the size of the cliff: over the next few years a slew of blockbuster patents expire. Brand-name drugs worth over $174B in annual sales lose exclusivity by 2032, with some estimates of lost sales as high as $350B. For the companies that weren't early movers in the GLP-1 gold rush — Merck, Pfizer, AbbVie — that's existential. Hence the race to ship new medicines even if it means betting on esoteric treatments
  • Lilly comes full circle: before Mounjaro and Zepbound, its last major growth spurt was Prozac, roughly 40 years ago. Lilly's chief scientific officer told CNBC that the stigma around mental health treatment then resembles the stigma around psychedelic therapy now. The regulatory environment has turned friendly — the FDA fast-tracked psychedelic research earlier this year. Lilly has said it would spend up to $25B on acquisitions this year (recent buys span vaccines, cancer and sleep medicine)
💡 Big-picture link

This is structurally identical to PayPal at $53B (Jul 15) — when a cash cow whose story has ended hits a deadline, capital buys time through M&A. In AI it was IBM; in fintech, PayPal; in pharma, the patent cliff is the clock. And it's telling that this landed the same day UnitedHealth beat on "AI-driven efficiency gains" — in healthcare, AI monetised as cost-cutting long before it monetised as a drug.

AI Goes Global
(7/16) AI goes international — Apple clears China's approval list, Nvidia works Japan, and the unicorns are Indian
Brew Markets · Morning Brew ▾
Investors hear the same names over and over — Nvidia, SpaceX, OpenAI. But while Wall Street obsesses over Silicon Valley, some of the most interesting developments are happening an ocean away.
  • Apple got through China's door (+1.76%): shares rose after the company was added to China's list of approved generative AI providers — clearing another hurdle in one of its most important markets and strengthening its ability to compete with domestic rivals. This week both Alibaba and Baidu announced AI partnerships with Apple: Qwen will power Apple Intelligence across iPhone, iPad, Mac and Vision Pro, while Baidu's search and AI technology will support Apple's AI features in China
  • Nvidia's Japan push: on Wednesday it argued Japan's AI market is rapidly maturing, unveiling the robotics AI model Cosmos 3 Edge and announcing partnerships with Fujitsu, Hitachi and Kawasaki Heavy Industries to expand its physical AI ecosystem. It also highlighted the expansion of Tokyo-1, Japan's AI drug-discovery consortium, with Astellas, Daiichi Sankyo and Ono Pharmaceutical aboard. Context: Microsoft committed $10B to Japanese AI infrastructure in April, and the country's AI market is projected to reach nearly $28B by 2029
  • India is quietly minting winners: vibe-coding startup Emergent became the country's second AI unicorn in a month, after Sarvam AI hit $1.5B. Roughly 12 million apps have already been built on Emergent's platform. IDC expects nearly half of Indian organisations to adopt AI cloud services this year, and Bank of America reports India is now the largest market for adoption of Gemini, ChatGPT and Perplexity
  • How to play it: as companies hunt their next leg of growth they'll look beyond their own shores and increasingly need local partners to navigate regulations, reach customers and tailor AI to each country. Apple needed Alibaba and Baidu for China; Nvidia is leaning on Japanese manufacturers and drugmakers to expand beyond data centers. The industry has long centred on the US, China and South Korea — it's expanding fast, and the little-known companies powering the next phase are worth watching
💡 Big-picture link

Apple's run is now a four-peat — Jul 13 the money it didn't spend → Jul 15 Qwen and a record high → Jul 16 Siri's "Signal" verdict → today Chinese regulatory approval. The larger implication: the definition of "the AI trade" is changing. This story, the US handing the UAE chips as a war reward (Jul 15), and Micron −8% on China's CXMT (Jul 16) are one story: AI is now a function of geography, regulation and alliances, not just technology. And the semiconductor trade BofA calls the most crowded ever hasn't priced that expansion in.

Price War
(7/17) Two cheap open-weight models in 48 hours — "the most attractive feature might not be capability, but bang for buck"
Tech Brew · WSJ ▾
Two cheap open-weight models dropped in the last 48 hours — Thinking Machines' Inkling and Moonshot AI's Kimi K3. It's a sign AI is becoming more of a buyer's market where consumers can shop around for the best price — and it's putting pressure on the legacy labs.
  • Inkling — the first model from an ex-OpenAI crew led by former CTO Mira Murati. The selling point isn't capability: while it isn't as capable as the top dogs, it uses just a fraction of its total parameters on a single task to stay faster and cheaper to run
  • Kimi K3 (Chinese) — more powerful, and topping some AI leaderboards in testing. It's the largest open-weight model ever released, with some analysts likening its performance to top US models such as Opus 4.8 — while being about 40% cheaper
  • The number underneath: the average price of an AI token has fallen by about 90% since 2023, as hardware and software improvements made tasks cheaper to run (though high-end models are generally a lot more token-hungry)
  • Why open weights capture that drop: they can run locally on your own machine or through a variety of third-party cloud providers competing to offer the lowest price, and they're more fine-tunable. Closed-weight incumbents can charge a markup because you can only get the models through them
  • The US labs' spicier explanation for cheap Chinese models: they accuse them of distilling proprietary American models to skip the expensive research and training phase
  • ⚠️ This is awkward for anyone heading into an IPO: OpenAI and Anthropic offer budget models too, but what they spend most of their energy promoting are the pricey, cutting-edge flagships — especially ahead of their IPOs. Yet OpenAI is reportedly weighing big price cuts to draw in customers (WSJ), and Anthropic has repeatedly kicked the can on switching Fable 5 to costlier usage-based pricing — perhaps a sign it's worried about attracting enough paying users to its top-tier model
  • Tech Brew's bottom line: an era where the most attractive AI feature might not be raw capability, but bang for buck. For those who don't need a top-of-the-line Lambo, there are plenty of reliable Camrys on the lot now
💡 Big-picture link

The warning this report wrote on May 1 has arrived exactly as written. Reading Big Tech's earnings then, we noted the pivot from "the best model" to "a marketplace hosting every model" and concluded: "AI gets commoditized → pricing power falls → the trillion-dollar valuations on OpenAI's and Anthropic's IPOs need revisiting." Two and a half months later, OpenAI is weighing cuts and Anthropic is postponing an increase. And where this meets Jul 15's BofA survey is the frightening part: the demand logic under the world's most crowded trade (semis) was "AI models sell at a premium" — and that premise is eroding. This is the supply-side sequel to the Apr 30 OpenAI shock.

AI Fallout
(7/17) Gemini's flagship slips again · Apple's suit expands to ~40 people · Netflix admits gen AI touched ~300 titles
Tech Brew ▾
The "delayed AI model" that knocked Alphabet down 4.43% the day before now has a name — Gemini's next flagship, which was supposed to arrive last month, has slipped again. The same day, Apple and Netflix each got their own version of AI's bill.
  • Gemini: for anyone waiting on the next flagship — "we have some bad news." The model was supposed to arrive last month. So Jul 16's −4.43% wasn't a one-off stumble but accumulated slippage
  • Apple's trade secrets suit got bigger: the company has reportedly sent legal letters to around 40 former employees. From the Jul 13 filing (alleging 400+ defections) to naming individuals and pressing them directly
  • Netflix came clean: it revealed that gen AI touched about 300 of its titles this year, and it's naming (some) names. That lands the same week it fell 9% after hours on a narrowed 2026 revenue forecast and cut its "What We Watched" report to once a year — one company disclosing less and more at the same time
  • The world's first humanoid robot free-combat tournament kicked off in China — including a robot that kept fighting after losing its head and one taunting with popular dances. Read it next to Jul 16's $2.4B Noetra item: China is already running its robots in live competition
  • The culture front: on the day Nolan's The Odyssey ($250M budget) opened, a startup teased "Odysseus: The Fall," a fully AI-generated 135-minute adaptation made for "mid-five figures." Its creator openly says he hopes to ride Nolan's coattails — while Nolan recently praised Gen Z for "utterly rejecting" AI slop
💡 Big-picture link

Three things point the same way — Google can't ship (delays), Apple is fighting in court (40 letters), and Netflix admits it already used AI on 300 titles. AI has moved from "when does it arrive" to "it's already here, and who is accountable." That's precisely the axis of Jul 16's xAI suing its own user and China's chatbot rules — and Nolan's "Gen Z is utterly rejecting it" is the cultural expression of that same social-licence limit.

Robotics
(7/16) Japan puts $2.4B into humanoids — but China's edge turned out to be scale, not brains
Finimize ▾
As part of a national humanoid robot push, Japan is pouring $2.4 billion into a new venture rallying its biggest tech and industrial champions. The project, Noetra, brings together SoftBank and Fujitsu among others to build an AI model for humanoid robots, while a parallel alliance of industrial heavyweights works with Nvidia to develop its robot AI toolkit.
  • Why Japan is in a hurry: it dominated industrial robotics for decades, but modern AI left it trailing the US and China. And the fight is personal — its workforce is shrinking and labor shortages are chronic, so a few million robot coworkers would really help. The prize: a global AI robotics market tipped to reach $5 trillion a year by 2050
  • China's real edge — scale, not better AI brains: China pumped $15 billion into humanoid startups in the first half of 2026 alone and deployed roughly 300,000 industrial robots to factory floors last year — versus just 38,000 in the US. The government also funded 64 data centers to train robots in real-life scenarios, from supermarket aisles to office corridors. That's millions of robot hours at a scale no fragmented private-sector effort can match
  • Nvidia could be the real victor: it is practically the sole maker of the advanced chips humanoid AI runs on — Noetra has already ordered 27,500 of them. Nvidia's stock has lagged other big AI names this year, but a genuine humanoid boom could put it back on the leaderboard
💡 Big-picture link

This is the flip side of China's Q2 GDP (Jul 15) — the "global demand for AI hardware and green tech" that drove exports +27% lives here, and so does the reason factories zoom while domestic investment falls 5.7%. And demographics are pushing the two countries in opposite directions: China banned chatbot romance over its birthrate (Jul 16), while Japan pours public money into robots over its shrinking workforce. Same population crisis, opposite prescriptions.

Energy / Power
(7/16) Energy IPOs hit their biggest haul since 1999 — and two-thirds of the listings trade below issue price
Finimize ▾
Traders hunting new ways to cash in on the AI boom are turning to the companies literally powering it — and energy firms are now raising record amounts through IPOs.
  • The size of the demand: a single AI data center burns roughly 876,000 megawatt-hours a year — about what it takes to power every household in Salt Lake City. And US electricity use is expected to jump 39% between now and 2035, with data centers accounting for a hefty chunk
  • The supply response: energy IPOs raked in $12.6 billion in the first half alone — the biggest first-half haul on record and the strongest six-month stretch since the dotcom peak in 1999. That's almost three times the $4.3 billion raised in all of 2025
  • ⚠️ Caveat emptor: IPOs are great for the banks running them (see this week's bumper earnings) — not necessarily for investors. Nearly two-thirds of the energy firms that listed this year and last now trade below their IPO prices, against just 40% across all sectors. Partly because some are built on technologies — nuclear fusion among them — that are still commercially unproven
  • Finimize's rules of thumb: buy at the listing price, favour VC-backed firms with over $100M in annual revenue, better still if already profitable, and remember bigger businesses tend to fare better than smaller, riskier ones — sizable tech debuts generally deliver the strongest post-listing returns
💡 Big-picture link

This and New York's first-in-the-nation data-center freeze (Jul 14) are two faces of the same bottleneck — power became a legal constraint and the hottest trade at the same moment. Put "two-thirds below issue price" next to SpaceX breaking its own IPO price this week and the picture sharpens: the phase where anything touching AI could list is giving way to the phase where it has to prove itself afterwards.

Prediction Markets
(7/16–17) Two faces of prediction markets — a teleprompter operator made six figures on inside information, and Kalshi opens betting on clinical trials
Brew Markets · Morning Brew ▾
The CFTC found that Gabriel Perez, President Trump's teleprompter operator since 2016, earned over six figures betting on Trump's speeches. His venue: Kalshi's so-called "Mentions" markets, where users bet on whether a politician will say a specific word, phrase or subject.
  • The irony: Trump himself has acknowledged he goes off the teleprompter "about 80% of the time" — as the Brew put it, good odds to bet on. Perez could perhaps have made even more
  • The same day, Kalshi announced it's expanding biotech wagers to include the outcomes of clinical trials. The Brew's question: a good way to hedge your pharma investments, or a symptom of the dystopic gamification of financial markets?
  • Why this matters this week: on Jul 15, Authers cited Kalshi's own Hormuz contract to argue that the ability to break down perceived risks and watch them change over time is very useful. He paired it with the worry that prediction markets are a covert way to bring gambling and sports betting to a wider audience. This is the first concrete evidence for the worry — a market where insiders can trade is also contaminated as a price signal
  • Political risk alongside it: ahead of the midterms, Trump used a primetime televised address to make many accusations about the readiness of the US voting system and the integrity of the 2020 results, reiterating previously disproven fraud claims — while not raising doubts about 2016 or 2024. Major outlets including NBC and ABC declined to air it live, offering it on streaming instead. He announced the declassification of 2020 election documents and urged passage of the SAVE America Act, stalled in the Senate
💡 Big-picture link

Given this report leaned on Kalshi's Hormuz odds on Jul 15, this is a warning about our own instrument. Prediction markets are useful only when they aggregate public information — the moment the teleprompter operator is trading, it isn't prediction, it's insider dealing. And opening bets on clinical trial outcomes transplants that structure onto the pharma patent cliff above. While the bottom of the K buys groceries with credit card points, the top bets on someone else's depression trial — this is how the fuel for Dimon's "anti-rich" resentment gets manufactured.

July 15–16, 2026
Wed–Thu — the trap inside "inflation has peaked," fund managers all-in on no landing, PayPal for $53B
Jul 15
PPI / Inflation
(7/15) PPI −0.3% prompts "inflation has peaked" — but strip out energy and every other component was rising
Bloomberg · John Authers / Brew Markets · Morning Brew · Finimize ▾
A day after CPI fell 0.4%, June producer prices dropped 0.3%, also below expectations — and again thanks to falling energy. Two days of good news was enough for New York Fed President John Williams to declare that "inflation has peaked," while NEC Director Kevin Hassett insisted "there's no excuse for raising rates right now." The 10-year slipped to 4.545% (−4.0bps).
  • Authers' objection — the data is frozen at June 30: this week's deluge of figures is a snapshot of the moment the Iran war was winding down and Hormuz traffic was steadily normalising. Kierkegaard: life can only be understood backwards, but it must be lived forwards. The problem is that the year's midpoint coincided almost perfectly with a turn in the oil price
  • The trap inside PPI: the headline improved sharply after a menacing spike — but excluding energy, the trend across every other component was clearly upward, which was not true of consumer prices. With energy costs rising again, the question of whether businesses must eventually pass higher prices on to consumers returns to the agenda
  • The Fed's minutes confirmed a hawkish tilt, but the inflation data that followed suggests expectations of an aggressive policy shift may have been overdone. A single month's print is not a trend
  • Authers: cooling inflation means lower bond yields and easier monetary conditions, which can be self-fulfilling positive prophecies. But if those conditions are easing on faulty assumptions drawn from backward-looking data, they only contribute to a future take-off in inflation
💡 Big-picture link

Capital Economics' line on Jul 14 CPI — a hike is a question of when, not whether — was reconfirmed by PPI within a day. June's data was good because oil fell in June; oil has risen again in July. Every piece of good news this week is past tense — and the market is stacking present-day positions on top of it.

Positioning / BofA
(7/15) Fund managers went all-in on "no landing" — cash below 4% (a sell signal), semis the most crowded trade ever recorded
Bloomberg · John Authers ▾
Bank of America's global fund manager survey landed. Respondents were interviewed July 2–9 — exactly as the Middle East began to veer out of control. And they were their most bullish on the world economy since the post-pandemic inflation surge. A majority now expects neither a soft nor a hard landing: the economy simply powers on.
  • Expressed in money, not words: that optimism showed up as very bullish equity positioning. Cash holdings dropped below 4% — a level BofA usually treats as a "sell" signal. They did it just as international events handed them their own excuse to sell
  • The optimism rests on oil: managers got this bullish on the assumption crude finishes the year between $70 and $80. That matched market pricing until today — Brent for January delivery now trades a few pennies over $80. Authers: recent events don't negate last month's positive views, but they clearly suggest the optimism wasn't fully grounded
  • The most crowded "most crowded" trade ever: over 80% of managers named semiconductors the world's most crowded trade — there has never been such strong agreement before. Authers reads this as good news: the world's most powerful money managers are aware of the risk. There's been some correction this month, with MSCI's semis index falling while the hyperscalers recovered a little — but the trade remains blatantly crowded
  • ⚠️ How no landing ends: Authers — the no-landing hypothesis generally ends with inflation and higher rates, and it's generally those higher rates that end equity bull markets. That, though, is still months in the future
💡 Big-picture link

This survey has exactly the same disease as the PPI story above — a worldview frozen at June 30. People who assumed $70–80 oil and ran cash below 4% are now looking at $80 January Brent. Combine it with Papic's "oil is an independent variable" (Jul 13) and the picture completes: the real risk here is the day this positioning unwinds itself.

Hormuz / Oil
(7/15) US strikes resume, Iran threatens to close more export routes — odds of normal traffic this year fall below 50/50
Bloomberg · John Authers / Morning Brew · Finimize ▾
With Hormuz tensions still escalating, the US reimposed its blockade of Iranian ships and launched another round of strikes aimed at degrading Iran's ability to attack commercial vessels. Earlier the same day, Iran's Revolutionary Guard warned the US to expect "other oil and gas export routes that serve the interests of the United States and its allies to be closed as well." Brent hit $85.94 (+1.43%); WTI $80.32 (+1.24%).
  • Prediction markets capitulated: Kalshi's contract on normal Hormuz traffic returning by year-end has slipped below 50/50, with odds falling sharply this month. Authers: prediction markets have always been more bearish on the Mideast than the financial markets linked to it. There are reasons to be alarmed by their growth as covert sports betting — but the ability to break down perceived risks and watch them change over time is very useful
  • Papic's logic, round three: the arrow of causation between oil and the blockade points both ways — higher prices redouble efforts to find a compromise, while the sharp drop into end-June emboldened Iran and the US to return to hostilities
  • Authers' base case: the primal desire on both sides for basic survival will probably limit escalation from here and disaster will be avoided — but oil prices will remain elevated. $80 January Brent is 33% above where it traded at the start of this year
  • ⚠️ Still unlikely but not excluded: a significant deterioration involving destruction of oil infrastructure — in which case every forecast needs rewriting
  • Collateral effect: the US granted the UAE access to coveted AI chips as a reward for supporting the US during its war with Iran — the conflict is now redrawing the map of the AI supply chain
💡 Big-picture link

Jul 13's 20% toll → reversed in 24 hours → blockade resumed → strikes → Iran's widening threat. Everything since June's MOU has been one more rung on the escalation ladder. And where this meets the two stories above is the crux: the market is trading June's peace while reality is July's war.

China
(7/15) China grows 4.3% in Q2, its slowest in three years — factories zooming, the domestic economy stuck in traffic
Finimize · Morning Brew ▾
China's economy grew just 4.3% in the second quarter — its weakest pace in three years and below the government's 4.5–5% target range. But the single number misleads, because China is running on two tracks.
  • 🔺 Factories are zooming, fuelled by global demand for AI hardware and green tech — June exports jumped 27% year-on-year, the strongest rise since 2021
  • 🔻 The domestic economy is stuck: consumer spending is weak, property is still sluggish, and business investment is dropping fast — funding for new factories, roads and buildings fell 5.7% in the first half
  • ⚠️ The catch in that export boom: exporters have been racing to ship before July 24th, when a temporary 10% US tariff expires and higher duties are expected. That may have inflated the figures — so the boom could fade next quarter
  • Out of deflation — but for the wrong reason: China's broadest inflation measure turned positive last quarter for the first time since early 2023, ending nearly three years of falling prices. On paper that's great — escaping deflation eases hefty mortgages and local government debts and revives corporate revenues. But the turnaround owes more to higher energy costs from the Iran war than to any genuine recovery in homegrown demand
  • Pressure is building for meaningful stimulus at policymakers' meeting later this month: cheaper borrowing, bigger infrastructure budgets, property support, tax cuts, subsidies, bailouts
💡 Big-picture link

Even China's exit from deflation was manufactured by Hormuz. Every macro print this week converges on a single variable: oil — US CPI/PPI looked good because oil fell; Chinese inflation turned positive because oil rose. Same cause, opposite readings. And half of that +27% in exports is future borrowed from the July 24 tariff deadline.

M&A / Fintech
(7/15) Stripe and Advent bid $53B for PayPal — the stock jumps 17.2%, Burry says it's "simply too low"
Brew Markets · Finimize · Morning Brew ▾
Private equity firm Advent International and privately held fintech Stripe offered to buy PayPal for $53 billion — $60.50 per share, a 28% premium to the previous close. PayPal shares rose 17.2%. If it goes through, Stripe and Advent would hold equal stakes. Financing is already lined up; there's no word yet on whether PayPal is even entertaining the offer.
  • How PayPal got cheap enough to buy: it was one of the pandemic's big winners, an investor favourite on the online shopping boom. Then rates rose in 2022, triggering a harsh correction across high-flying tech — and PayPal couldn't stick the landing, hurt by stagnating user growth and fierce competition. Shares have meandered lower ever since and were down 19% this year. The analyst consensus is a "Hold" with a median target of $47. The stock fetched $73 a year ago and is miles off its 2021 peak
  • Why Stripe wants it: Stripe is the plumbing of B2B payments — used by 90% of the Dow and 80% of the Nasdaq 100. It processed $1.9 trillion last year (+34%) and was valued at $159 billion in February (up from $106.5B). What it lacks is exactly what PayPal has: a deep consumer base, distribution and brand. PayPal moves ~$2 trillion a year across ~439 million accounts (Stripe: 250 million) and owns Venmo. Combined, roughly $3.7 trillion a year would flow through the pair
  • Michael Burry dissents: the $53 billion offer is "simply too low" and PayPal is "one of the cheapest quality businesses in the portfolio" — he isn't selling into the pop
  • Timing problem: PayPal's new CEO was about to cut $1.5 billion of costs and reorganise into three operating units. The bid may kill the reboot before it starts
  • Fintech is consolidating: last week the WSJ reported US banks were looking at buying Fiserv's debit-card network to dodge federal debit-fee caps. Soon it may not matter whether you use PayPal, Venmo or Zelle — different logos, far fewer companies behind the curtain
💡 Big-picture link

This is the shadow side of the "AI capex supercycle" confirmed in Jul 14's bank earnings: while capital stampedes toward AI, quality cash cows without an AI story get left at giveaway prices — and PE and strategic buyers pick them up. It's the M&A version of IBM −25%.

Rotation
(7/15) SpaceX dips below its IPO price for the first time · Micron −8% on CXMT · Apple hits a record on Alibaba's Qwen
Brew Markets · Morning Brew · Finimize ▾
All three major indexes closed higher — Nasdaq 26,269.23 (+0.62%), S&P 7,572.4 (+0.38%), Dow 52,658.64 (+0.29%). What matters is the picture beneath the index: investors moved money out of chip stocks and into Big Tech. Six of the Magnificent Seven rose, and that flow carried the tape.
  • Apple +4.01% to a new all-time high — on news that Alibaba's Qwen model will power Apple Intelligence features in China. Alibaba gained 4.78%. Jul 13's "the company that didn't spend won" story now has a China answer bolted on
  • SpaceX −0.6%, briefly below its IPO price for the first time, closing back above it at $135.27. The first psychological line of defence for a $1.78T valuation and $85B raise has now been breached
  • Micron −8.02% — as Chinese chipmaker ChangXin Memory Technologies (CXMT) moved closer to an IPO, raising competition concerns. That is the first supply-side crack in the memory supercycle
  • Earnings: BlackRock +6.63% on a beat and on becoming the first investment firm past $15 trillion in AUM ($15.34T; $192B of quarterly inflows), with Larry Fink pointing to steadier private credit. BNY Mellon +5.12%; Morgan Stanley +0.39% after a 69% surge in equities trading drove record Q2 revenue and profit. ASML lifted its 2026 revenue target to €43–45 billion and said EUV tool capacity through 2027 is already mostly booked
  • The other side: Progressive −9.43% (June net income −31%), Elevance Health −9.27% (Medicaid worries), Pentair −15%, J&J −2.69% (weak Stelara sales), Celcuity −17.6%. Up: Lionsgate +5.04% (Bolloré Group interest in a sale), Aehr Test Systems +21.91%
  • Wall Street's new fashion: JPMorgan, BlackRock and Goldman are all jumping onto the tokenization bandwagon
💡 Big-picture link

Two signals landed the same day — SpaceX broke its IPO price, and Micron fell 8% on Chinese competition. If Jul 14's "IBM −25% vs Hynix +27%" was money moving inside AI, today the edges of the AI trade itself started to fray. This is precisely where BofA's "semis are the most crowded trade ever" bites.

Dollar / FX
(7/15) The dollar's test — a war-driven revival meets structural forces reasserting themselves
Bloomberg · John Authers (Richard Abbey) ▾
The dollar is the traditional safe haven for geopolitical uncertainty, and the Iran war drove its recovery this year. Now comes a test of its staying power, because the structural forces weighing on the greenback look set to reassert themselves.
  • Expectations reversing: the Fed's minutes confirmed a hawkish tilt, but the inflation data that followed suggests expectations of a more aggressive policy shift were overdone. One month's print isn't a trend, but it calls the dollar's revival into fresh question
  • The bond market already answered: Treasuries are behaving as though concerns about Fed tightening were overstated. There's a tight relationship between relative bond yields and currency performance, and the narrowing advantage of US yields over the rest of the world points to a dollar already losing momentum — with further downside likely if easing expectations solidify
  • What the survey says: a strong majority of BofA respondents think the dollar is overvalued and see little further upside even with geopolitical risk very much alive — which, if anything, implies an expectation that this escalation will be short-lived
  • BCA Research's Artem Sakhbiev: a sustained breakout in oil could still provide near-term support and put a floor under the currency, even if the broader uptrend has run its course. But "seasonality tends to be quite negative for the dollar in the third quarter, and if we see some steepening in the yield curve… those could be near-term headwinds. That's why we are more comfortable being on the sidelines."
  • HSBC's Max Kettner: a strong Q2 earnings season could be a tailwind — with US banks off to a solid start, the reporting season may prove even more consequential for US assets than this week's inflation data. How much reaches the dollar is less clear
💡 Big-picture link

Jul 13's "bring the $1.6T GPIF money home" and this story are two halves of one thing — while the war props the dollar up, the weaponisation of capital flows is digging underneath it. And here the week's irony completes: good inflation news is dollar-weakening news, which in turn raises import prices.

K-Shape / Retirement
(7/15) 69% say retirement is out of reach for their whole generation — and why AI's wealth effect is holding the economy up
Brew Markets ▾
Asset manager Schroders asked 1,500 investors in March and April how much is enough to retire. The answer: about $1.2 million. The rub: only 30% think they'll even reach the $1 million mark.
  • What they actually expect to have: more than half (51%) plan on less than $500,000; 24% say they'll fall below $250,000. All told, 69% say retirement is simply out of reach for their entire generation
  • They blame prices: 55% said saving 10% of their paycheques was impossible against competing expenses; 27% have curbed contributions, 70% of them within the past two years; and 27% have borrowed from their retirement funds
  • So why does the economy look fine? The wealth effect: rising asset prices coinciding with rising spending has driven about one-third of the increase in consumer expenditures since the pandemic. The rule of thumb: every $1.00 rise in a household's stock portfolio boosts spending by $0.05. Multiply that by the many millions minted in Anthropic, Micron and other tech names, and it becomes clear why the economy seems fine while hiding serious cracks underneath
  • Dimon, round two: JPMorgan's CEO says the widening divide has sparked a fresh wave of "anti-rich" resentment, and he doesn't blame people for being annoyed — "If you were the average citizen here and you say, 'These wealthy people are getting unbelievably wealthy, and this segment's been left behind,' that's kind of annoying."
💡 Big-picture link

This may be what "no landing" actually is: the economy keeps going not because everyone is fine, but because the top of the K spends alongside the index and covers for the bottom's contraction. If that spending is bolted to AI share prices at five cents on the dollar, then the day the most crowded trade unwinds, consumption unwinds with it. That is exactly the tectonic plate Dimon described on Jul 14.

OpenAI / China
(7/16) OpenAI's first device is a screenless speaker designed to feel "alive" — the same week China ordered users to dump their bots
Morning Brew · Finimize · Tech Brew ▾
The first device from the maker of ChatGPT will be an Alexa competitor designed to seem alive, Bloomberg reported. The same week, China issued the exact opposite instruction — disable the features that make users emotionally dependent on chatbots.
  • The device: due next year, a screenless smart speaker carrying ChatGPT's latest, most conversational Voice Mode, plus moving "mechanical elements" meant to create "a sense that it is alive." A camera and sensors help it understand the user's surroundings and context; a rechargeable battery invites you to carry it room to room. It becomes more personalised and proactive over time by reading your digital information, like emails
  • What could derail it: last week Apple sued OpenAI for stealing trade secrets through some of the 400+ former Apple employees who defected — and OpenAI's hardware effort is staffed with ex-Apple designers and engineers. OpenAI denies it. Also on the roadmap: home robotics, an AI necklace, and a smartphone-like device — but the lawsuit could reset the timeline
  • China's opposite order: new rules took effect requiring tech firms to disable features that draw users into emotionally dependent relationships with AI, in the hope of pushing a dwindling population to have children again. China's population fell for a fourth straight year in 2025 and its birthrate hit its lowest level since 1949. ByteDance, Alibaba and Tencent's chatbots can no longer tell users everything they say is cute; minors are banned from bot relationships and pre-release oversight tightens
  • Why the urgency — Tencent Research Institute's April survey of 18-to-40-year-olds: over 70% had developed a dependence on AI, nearly 80% had felt "AI understands me", and on difficult subjects 56% turned to AI while only 14% confided in a fellow human
💡 Big-picture link

On the same technology, a US company is betting money on "more alive" while a government is legislating "less alive." Since the Apr 30 OpenAI shock the axis of competition has been model capability; it is now shifting to devices (distribution) and regulation (social tolerance). And the Apple suit extends Jul 13: Apple didn't spend on AI — it litigates instead.

AI Liability
(7/16) xAI sues its own user — the first time an AI lab has sued a customer, and the "neutral tool" defence
Tech Brew · WSJ ▾
xAI has sued a man for allegedly using Grok to make child sexual abuse material — likely the first time an AI lab has sued one of its own users over how they used its product. The irony: xAI is doing this while facing a wave of lawsuits blaming its model for generating nonconsensual sexual imagery.
  • A very Musk opening: the 12-page complaint begins with a screenshot of his own January 2026 X post — "Anyone using Grok to make illegal content will suffer the same consequences as if they upload illegal content."
  • The defendant is 67-year-old Terry Harwood, accused of "repeated, deliberate, and unconscionable violations" of the terms by bypassing safeguards. The load-bearing claim: Grok is a "neutral tool, subject to user control," and Harwood "designed misleading prompts" to make it comply
  • ⚠️ The awkward part: this argument comes from the company that shipped "Spicy Mode" for explicit image and video generation last August, flooding X with nonconsensual deepfakes. Per a Center for Countering Digital Hate analysis, Grok created roughly 3 million sexualised images — including of children — over just 11 days from December 2025 to January
  • The precedent: the closest case is Microsoft's 2024 suit against a cybercrime ring that stole API keys and hacked the DALL-E generator offered through Azure. But there's no evidence Harwood hacked Grok — meaning xAI is assigning liability to a user of built-in features
  • xAI is fighting deepfake lawsuits in multiple countries; Harwood already faces criminal charges in South Carolina. xAI wants him to cover its own financial losses if his alleged victims come after the company too
  • The social temperature: per the WSJ, violent threats against AI companies are rising and have executives "fearing for their lives." On the other side, George Lucas says AI sceptics are like people clinging to "the horse and the buggy"
💡 Big-picture link

This week's AI axis isn't capability — it's liability and social licence: China outlawed emotional-dependency features, xAI is trying to push responsibility onto users, and executives are worrying about their safety. It matters because of the balance sheet: if harm caused by a model is the user's fault, AI capex is a safe investment; if it's the company's, it's a liability nobody has booked yet. Same bedrock as Dimon's "anti-rich" resentment on Jul 15.

Apple / Anthropic
(7/16) The last piece of the Apple story — a month with Siri AI returns a verdict of "Signal," and Anthropic edges closer to IPO
Tech Brew · Morning Brew ▾
Tech Brew ran the new agentic Siri daily since the developer beta landed on June 11 and delivered its verdict: Signal. "Worth the wait — mostly." The same day brought a report that Anthropic is one step closer to its IPO.
  • The good: the on-device experience, not the app, is the star — a Dynamic Island interface, a slick speaking waveform, and visual cards instead of walls of text. The best use of the agentic features is search: asked for a friend's address texted months earlier and never saved, Siri dug it out of the messages. Same for finding particular photos
  • The complicated: its agentic reach stops at Apple's own apps for now. It couldn't buy Broadway tickets in TodayTix ("I can't search within the TodayTix app right now"), and asked to erase strangers from a photo it merely opened the Clean Up tool. The bad: occasionally the loading swirl vanished with no answer and no record of the question
  • The verdict that matters: "It won't unseat ChatGPT, Claude, or Gemini as your do-everything chatbot, but those can't rummage through your texts and camera roll as seamlessly. For the things that live on your phone, this is the most useful Siri has ever been." — Apple's battlefield is not the model, it's data access
  • Timing: beta signups opened Monday on the iOS 27 public beta; official release with iOS 27 in September, free, but only on iPhone 15 Pro or newer
  • Also: Anthropic is reportedly one step closer to its IPO (extending the Goldman/Morgan Stanley underwriting line from Jul 14) · AppleCare+ is getting a price hike for Macs and iPads too — inflation arriving as service fees rather than device prices · the NTSB's preliminary report says the driver overrode the self-driving software in last month's fatal Texas Tesla crash
💡 Big-picture link

Jul 13 "the money it didn't spend is the earnings" → Jul 15 Alibaba's Qwen solves China, stock hits a record → today confirmation the product actually works. Apple's hat-trick is complete — and how it won is the point: not by building the best model, but by bolting a model onto data it already had, while protecting $140B of free cash flow. Anthropic's IPO is the opposite route: hand the capex bill to the public market.

EV / Credit
(7/15) Lucid: −40% intraday, then +28.79% — an experiment in how fast markets will believe the worst
Brew Markets ▾
Shares of EV maker Lucid plunged as much as 40% intraday the previous day before closing down 16%, after reports that the company was weighing either going private or filing for Chapter 11. Then the stock rebounded 28.79% after Lucid called those reports "completely false."
  • Why the market believed it — the industry: EVs have been under pressure ever since the Trump administration eliminated the $7,500 federal tax credit for new EV purchases
  • Why the market believed it — the company: Lucid isn't expected to be cash-flow positive until 2029, recently missed Wall Street's delivery expectations, and announced plans to lay off 18% of its workforce
  • The rebuttal: "The company has sufficient liquidity to carry its operations well into next year, as recently published in its last quarterly filings, and it has not formed any special Board committee to explore the scenarios reported."
  • The balance sheet backs it: Wall Street expects Lucid to burn about $2.8 billion over the next 12 months, while it ended March with roughly $3.2 billion of liquidity and raised another $1 billion in April. It will, eventually, need to raise more to reach profitability
  • What remains: the reports were false, but they showed how quickly the market is willing to believe the worst. Next month's earnings need to give investors something more tangible to trade on
💡 Big-picture link

In one week, PayPal +17% on a rumour and Lucid −40% then +29%. The index is quiet while single-stock amplitude is extreme — which is exactly why Jul 14's bank trading revenues were the best on record. What this market is selling isn't direction, it's volatility, and every day supplies a fresh exhibit.

July 13–15, 2026
Mon–Wed — a 20% toll reversed in 24 hours, CPI's first fall, record bank profits
Jul 13
Hormuz / Oil
(7/13–14) Hormuz closed again → Trump's 20% toll → reversed within 24 hours — oil's biggest daily gain since 2020
Bloomberg · John Authers / Morning Brew · Brew Markets ▾
The US and Iran traded missiles over the weekend. The US hit 140 military targets on Saturday and dozens more on Sunday; Iran struck US allies across Qatar, Kuwait, Jordan, Oman and Bahrain. The trigger: Iran attacked a Cyprus-flagged container ship in the Strait. Tehran then said it had fully closed Hormuz again, and Brent rallied 5% at Monday's open to $79 (+12.7% off this month's low). By Tuesday crude topped $80 in its largest single-day gain since 2020.
  • Jul 13, Trump's gambit: he reimposed the naval blockade, declared the US the Strait's "guardian," and said it would charge 20% of cargo value to escort ships through. Bloomberg estimated that at roughly $30 million for a loaded supertanker — several times the ~$2 million Iran had been charging — against a norm of 2–3% of cargo value. The UN's IMO opposed it as contrary to international law
  • Jul 14, reversed in a day — replaced, Trump said, by "MASSIVE" Gulf investment in the US. The blockade of Iranian ports resumed anyway
  • Authers: June's Memorandum of Understanding was always ambiguous about exactly who controls the Strait and on what terms — and is now a dead letter. December Brent is more expensive than when the MOU was signed
  • The structural weak point: global oil inventories (public and private) sit at depths unseen since 1984 — making a long blockade progressively harder to sustain
  • BCA's Marko Papic: "oil prices are an independent variable, not a dependent one" — when prices are low, military activity ramps up; when they rise, negotiations return. So June's over-rapid fall in oil is what let the conflict re-escalate. And markets no longer treat the Strait as binary but as a continuum (the 1980s Tanker Wars model) — which limits the reaction
  • The spillover: fertilizer is the war's biggest casualty — Hormuz is a vital conduit for urea, and prices rose over 30% early in the conflict. Add El Niño crop risk and food inflation could turn sticky — the kind of inflation central banks can do nothing about
💡 Big-picture link

Authers' Memorandum of Misunderstanding from Jul 9 is now literally void. The deeper point is Papic's: markets create the facts. Cheap oil emboldens the fighting; expensive oil forces talks. This war cannot settle itself.

CPI / Warsh
(7/14) CPI fell for the first time since 2020 — and Warsh still refused to say "mission accomplished"
Bloomberg · John Authers / Brew Markets · Morning Brew ▾
June consumer prices fell 0.4% on the month — the first monthly decline since 2020, helped by the biggest drop in gasoline since 2022. Headline inflation eased to 3.5%, and core was flat on the month at 2.6% annually. A day earlier, Governor Waller had warned that "if we get another hot reading on core inflation this week, the FOMC will need to consider tightening" — pushing July hike odds to a coin flip. The reversal was dramatic.
  • The breadth: all four main components fell year-on-year. The Fed's supercore (services ex-housing) declined (though still above 3%), the Atlanta Fed's sticky-price gauge hit its lowest since the pandemic, and the Cleveland Fed's trimmed mean dipped below 3%
  • Oxford Economics: of the Fed's three inflation worries, tariff effects weren't discernible and AI-related price pressure wasn't as evident as expected; some oil pass-through showed up and may take longer to work through
  • The market: odds of a hold rose 58%→83% and hike odds fell 42%→16%. Per Deutsche Bank it was the second-largest repricing for the next meeting after a CPI release since 2008. One-year inflation swaps dropped below 2% for the first time since the 2024 election
  • Warsh's maiden congressional testimony: "There might be some who look at today's data and say 'mission accomplished.' That is not my view." · "I'm not for cherry picking" · and no commitment to hikes either. His framing: getting policy right is "the star we steer by"
  • ⚠️ But real yields are the highest since 2023, having surpassed even last year's Liberation Day peak. And with oil back up, July won't be another negative print — Capital Economics: a hike is a matter of when, not if
💡 Big-picture link

Authers called it "Reversal Day" — the toll and the hike scare both flipped within 24 hours. The direction, though, hasn't changed: inflation is still too high to cut, and the next move is still up. This is exactly why Warsh cut his statement to 130 words in June — you can't be forced to reverse a promise you never made.

Bank Earnings
(7/14) Five banks earned $49B in a quarter — JPMorgan's biggest profit in banking history, Goldman's best trading haul ever
Brew Markets · Morning Brew · Finimize · Bloomberg ▾
On "Super Tuesday," America's five biggest banks reported together — and the numbers were overwhelming: $49 billion in combined profit. The irony sits at the center: the money came from the volatility created by the Iran war and the AI panic.
  • JPMorgan: profit +41% to $21.2B — the highest in banking history (including a $4.6B one-off from selling its Visa stake). Goldman Sachs: +78% to $6.6B, with equities trading +72% to $7.4B, a Wall Street record. Bank of America: trading +70% to $3.62B (a company record), profit +27% to $9.1B. Citigroup: profit $5.8B and equities trading +45% — yet shares fell 5.27% on the gap to larger rivals. Wells Fargo: +17% to $6.4B
  • The engines: SpaceX's $85B IPO drew retail in droves and paid the banks ~$500 million, while the repeated opening and closing of Hormuz, plus the AI argument, generated the volatility they trade on
